❓:In triangle ABC, angle ABC is obtuse. Point D lies on side AC such that angle ABD is right, and point E lies on side AC between A and D such that BD bisects angle EBC. Find CE given that AC=35, BC=7, and BE=5.

We need to find CE given that AC = 35, BC = 7, and BE = 5.Let me start by drawing triangle ABC. Since angle ABC is obtuse, vertex B is the one with the angle greater than 90 degrees. For angle at B to be obtuse, in coordinate terms, the vectors BA and BC should form an angle greater than 90 degrees, which means their dot product is negative. BA is from B to A, which is (a, 0), and BC is from B to C, which is (c_x, c_y). The dot product is a*c_x + 0*c_y = a*c_x. For this to be negative, c_x must be negative. Therefore, point C should be to the left of the y-axis (since B is at (0,0)).So let's assign coordinates accordingly. Let’s set point B at (0,0), point A at (a, 0), and point C at (-d, e) where d > 0 and e > 0 (to make AC have positive length). Therefore, point D is at (0, (a e)/(a + d)).But BD is the segment from (0,0) to (0, (a e)/(a + d)), which is indeed vertical, hence perpendicular to AB. Alternatively, perhaps assume some integer values.Given that BC = 7, AC = 35, and BE = 5, the numbers might be chosen to result in integer lengths. Let's see if we can find integers a and d that satisfy a^2 + 2 a d = 1176 and d^2 + e^2 = 49.Wait, e^2 = 49 - d^2. So e must be a real number, so d^2 ≤ 49 => d ≤ 7. We need to find (CE) given (AC = 35), (BC = 7), and (BE = 5).1. Angle Bisector Theorem: Since (BD) bisects (angle EBC), by the angle bisector theorem, (frac{ED}{DC} = frac{BE}{BC} = frac{5}{7}).2. Parametric Coordinates: Place (A) at ((0, 0)), (B) at ((h, 0)), and (C) at ((p, q)). Using the given lengths (AC = 35) and (BC = 7), we derive the equations: [ p^2 + q^2 = 1225 quad text{and} quad (p - h)^2 + q^2 = 49 ]3. Solving for (h) and (p): Subtracting the second equation from the first, we get: [ 2hp - h^2 = 1176 ]4. Parameterization: Using the parameter (t) for points on (AC), we find (D) at (t = frac{h}{p}) and (E) at (t = s). Using the angle bisector theorem, we derive: [ frac{left(frac{h}{p} - sright)}{left(1 - frac{h}{p}right)} = frac{5}{7} ]5. Solving for (s): Solving the above equation with (k = frac{h}{p}), we find (k = frac{5}{6}) and (s = frac{5}{7}).6. Finding (CE): Since (E) is at (t = frac{5}{7}) on (AC), the length (CE) is: [ CE = left(1 - frac{5}{7}right) times 35 = frac{2}{7} times 35 = 10 ]Thus, the final answer is (boxed{10}).]

🔑:1. Define the angles and segments: Let alpha = angle CBD = angle EBD and beta = angle BAC. Also, let x = CE.2. Apply the Law of Sines in triangle ABE: [ frac{sin (90^circ - alpha)}{35 - x} = frac{sin beta}{5} ] Since sin (90^circ - alpha) = cos alpha, this equation becomes: [ frac{cos alpha}{35 - x} = frac{sin beta}{5} ]3. Apply the Law of Sines in triangle ABC: [ frac{sin (alpha + 90^circ)}{35} = frac{sin beta}{7} ] Since sin (alpha + 90^circ) = cos alpha, this equation becomes: [ frac{cos alpha}{35} = frac{sin beta}{7} ]4. Equate the expressions for frac{cos alpha}{sin beta}: From the first equation: [ frac{cos alpha}{sin beta} = frac{35 - x}{5} ] From the second equation: [ frac{cos alpha}{sin beta} = frac{35}{7} ]5. Set the two expressions equal to each other: [ frac{35 - x}{5} = frac{35}{7} ]6. Solve for x: [ frac{35 - x}{5} = 5 ] [ 35 - x = 25 ] [ x = 10 ]The final answer is boxed{10}.

🧠:[<think>Okay, let's see. The problem is about a calculator keyboard with digits 0-9 and two operation signs. The initial display is 0. Since the problem is about odd numbers, the operations' parity behavior will matter.Wait, if the operations are addition and multiplication, then the parity (odd/even) of the result depends on the operands and the operations. For example, adding an even number doesn't change parity, adding an odd number toggles it. Multiplying by an even number makes the result even, multiplying by an odd number preserves parity. But if the operations are different, like division and subtraction, it might complicate things. But since the problem says "signs of two operations", maybe the operations are unary or binary? Wait, but the calculator starts with 0, then you can press digits or operations. Adding an even number (50%): E + even = E (even + even = even). - Adding an odd number (50%): E + odd = O (even + odd = odd). - So next state: 50% E, 50% O.- If current state is O: - Adding an even number (50%): O + even = O (odd + even = odd). - Adding an odd number (50%): O + odd = E (odd + odd = even). - So next state: 50% O, 50% E.For multiplication (×):- If current state is E: - Multiplying by any number: - If the number is even (50%): E × even = E (even × even = even). - If the number is odd (50%): E × odd = E (even × odd = even). - So next state: 100% E.- If current state is O: - Multiplying by even (50%): O × even = E (odd × even = even). - Multiplying by odd (50%): O × odd = O (odd × odd = odd). - So next state: 50% E, 50% O.Now, since the operation is chosen with probability 50% for + and 50% for ×, we can compute the overall transition probabilities.From state E:- With 50% probability, operation is +, leading to 50% E, 50% O.- With 50% probability, operation is ×, leading to 100% E.Therefore, total transitions from E:- P(E → E) = 0.5 * 0.5 (from +, even number) + 0.5 * 1 (from ×) = 0.25 + 0.5 = 0.75- P(E → O) = 0.5 * 0.5 (from +, odd number) = 0.25From state O:- With 50% probability, operation is +, leading to 50% O, 50% E.- With 50% probability, operation is ×, leading to 50% E, 50% O.Therefore, transitions from O:- P(O → E) = 0.5 * 0.5 (from +, odd number) + 0.5 * 0.5 (from ×, even number) = 0.25 + 0.25 = 0.5- P(O → O) = 0.5 * 0.5 (from +, even number) + 0.5 * 0.5 (from ×, odd number) = 0.25 + 0.25 = 0.5So the transition matrix is:From E:- To E: 0.75- To O: 0.25From O:- To E: 0.5- To O: 0.5We can represent this as:``` E OE 0.75 0.25O 0.5 0.5```To find the steady-state probabilities, we need to solve the equations:π_E = 0.75 π_E + 0.5 π_Oπ_O = 0.25 π_E + 0.5 π_OWith π_E + π_O = 1From the first equation:π_E = 0.75 π_E + 0.5 π_O=> π_E - Addition (+): - Even + Even = Even - Even + Odd = Odd - Odd + Even = Odd - Odd + Odd = Even - Parity changes with 50% probability.2. Multiplication (×): - Even × Any = Even - Odd × Even = Even - Odd × Odd = Odd - Parity remains even if multiplied by an even number, and preserves parity if multiplied by an odd number.Each number has a 50% chance of being even or odd, and each operation (addition or multiplication) is equally likely. The transitions between states are calculated based on these probabilities:- From Even (E): - 50% chance of addition: leads to 50% E, 50% O. - 50% chance of multiplication: leads to 100% E. - Total transitions: 75% E, 25% O.- From Odd (O): - 50% chance of addition: leads to 50% E, 50% O. - 50% chance of multiplication: leads to 50% E, 50% O. - Total transitions: 50% E, 50% O.The steady-state probabilities are found by solving the system of equations for the Markov chain:- ( pi_E = 0.75 pi_E + 0.5 pi_O )- ( pi_O = 0.25 pi_E + 0.5 pi_O )- ( pi_E + pi_O = 1 )Solving these, we find:- ( pi_E = frac{2}{3} )- ( pi_O = frac{1}{3} )Thus, the approximate probability that the result is an odd number is (boxed{dfrac{1}{3}}).,<think>Okay, let's try to figure out this probability problem. Conclusion: - The probability that the result is odd is approximately 11/23.boxed{dfrac{11}{23}}]

🔑:We will say that the Scientist has performed n steps if he has entered n numbers, and between them, n-1 times performed some arithmetic operations. Let p_{n} denote the probability that after n steps, the number on the calculator is odd, and express p_{n+1} in terms of p_{n}.If the last operation was multiplication, the result will be odd only if both factors are odd; the probability of this is 1 / 2 p^{2}.If the last operation was addition, the result will be odd if the last addend differs in parity from the penultimate one; the probability of this is 1 / 2.The last operation can be either multiplication or addition, and both of these options are equally likely.Therefore, the formula for the total probability gives:p_{n+1}=1 / 2 cdot 1 / 2 + 1 / 2 cdot 1 / 2 p_{n} = 1 / 4 + 1 / 4 p_{n}.Rewriting this equality as p_{n+1} - 1 / 3 = 1 / 4 (p_{n} - 1 / 3), we see that when n increases by 1, the difference p_{n} - 1 / 3 decreases by a factor of 4. Therefore, p_{n} is close to 1 / 3 for large n.## Answer

Let's look for a formula or a method to compute this.Upon some research, I recall that when considering numbers with leading zeros, the sum can be calculated by considering the contribution of each digit to each place value, accounting for the fact that some permutations will result in numbers with fewer digits. Here's an approach:1. For each digit in the original number, calculate how many times it contributes to each place value (units, tens, hundreds, etc.) in the sum.2. For a digit d in the original number, the number of times it appears in the j-th position (from the right) of the sum is equal to the number of valid permutations where d is in the j-th position of the resulting number (after leading zeros are removed). However, determining this count is not straightforward. Let's think recursively. Suppose the original number has n digits, with some zeros. Let’s denote the number of non-zero digits as k. Then, the permutations can be categorized based on the number of leading zeros:- Permutations with 0 leading zeros: These are permutations starting with a non-zero digit. The number of such permutations is (k) * (n-1)! / (duplicate counts). Each of these permutations contributes as an n-digit number.- Permutations with 1 leading zero: These start with a zero followed by a non-zero digit. The number of such permutations is (number of zeros) * (k) * (n-2)! / (duplicate counts). These contribute as (n-1)-digit numbers.- Similarly, permutations with m leading zeros (m < number of zeros) would start with m zeros followed by a non-zero digit, and the rest permuted. These contribute as (n - m)-digit numbers.This seems like a promising approach. Let's formalize it.Let’s denote:- t: total number of zeros in the original number.- k: number of non-zero digits. So, n = t + k.- The digits are D = {0, 0, ..., 0, d_1, d_2, ..., d_k}, where d_i are non-zero.The total sum can be broken down into parts based on the number of leading zeros:For m = 0 to t (but m can't exceed n - 1, since after all, you need at least one non-zero digit):- Number of permutations with exactly m leading zeros: C(m) = (number of ways to choose m zeros) * (number of ways to arrange the remaining digits with the first non-zero digit followed by permutations).But since the zeros are indistinct, the number of ways to choose m zeros is 1 (since all zeros are the same). The formula is:[text{Sum} = left( frac{text{sum_digits} times text{num_permutations}}{m} right) times (10^m - 1) / 9]where ( m ) is the number of digits, (text{sum_digits}) is the sum of the digits, and (text{num_permutations}) is the number of unique permutations of the digits.Given the sum 4,933,284, we factorize it and find that ( m = 5 ) works. The quotient from the division is 444, leading to:[text{sum_digits} times text{num_permutations} = 2220]We need digits that sum to 37 with 5 digits and permutations count 60. The valid digits are 4, 7, 8, 9, 9, forming the smallest number 47899. Verifying the sum:[text{Sum} = left( frac{37 times 60}{5} right) times 11111 = 444 times 11111 = 4,933,284]Thus, the smallest number is boxed{47899}.]

🔑:Solution. In our problem, the number we are looking for cannot be a four-digit number or smaller, because from such a number, by rearranging its digits, we can obtain at most 24 different numbers, all of which are less than 10,000. Thus, the sum of these numbers is less than 240,000. Therefore, let's examine the five-digit numbers.If any digit in the number we are looking for appears at least three times, then the number of different numbers that can be obtained by permuting the digits is at most 20, and each of these numbers is less than 100,000. Thus, the sum of these numbers is less than 2,000,000. Therefore, this is also not possible.It is also not possible for two digits to each appear exactly twice, because from such a number, we can obtain 30 different numbers, the sum of which is less than (30 cdot 100000 = 3000000).Therefore, if there is a five-digit number that meets the conditions, then either all its digits are different, or exactly two of its digits are the same.First, let's consider the case where all digits are different. The number is of the form (overline{a b c d e}) (where (a, b, c, d), and (e) are all different digits). In this case, each digit appears 24 times in each place value when the digits are permuted. Thus, the sum of the numbers obtained is:[begin{aligned}& 24 cdot 11111 cdot a + 24 cdot 11111 cdot b + 24 cdot 11111 cdot c + 24 cdot 11111 cdot d + 24 cdot 11111 cdot e = & = 24 cdot 11111 cdot (a + b + c + d + e) = 4933284end{aligned}]from which (2 cdot (a + b + c + d + e) = 37) follows. This is clearly not possible, since the left side is an even number, while the right side is odd.Finally, let's see if there is a five-digit number where exactly two digits are the same and it meets the conditions. Among the permutations of the digits of such a number, there is, for example, one of the form (overline{a a b c d}), where (a, b, c), and (d) are different digits. If we fix one of the (a) digits in any place value, then by permuting the other digits, we get 24 numbers, so the (a) digit appears 24 times in each place value. If we fix any other digit in any place value, then the other digits have 12 different permutations, so each digit different from (a) appears 12 times in each place value.Thus, the sum of the numbers obtained by permuting the digits of the number (overline{a a b c d}) is: (24 cdot 11111 cdot a + 12 cdot 11111 cdot b + 12 cdot 11111 cdot c + 12 cdot 11111 cdot d = 4933284). Therefore, (2a + b + c + d = 37). We can then find the smallest five-digit number that meets this condition by choosing the smallest possible digit for the first place value. This is possible if the other place values have the largest possible digits; according to this, the largest possible digits are: (2 cdot 9 + 8 + 7 = 33), so the digit in the first place value must be 4.Therefore, there is a five-digit number among the five-digit numbers that satisfies the conditions (and among the smaller numbers, there is not), so the smallest such five-digit number is the smallest number we are looking for: 47899.

❓:Given a triangle ABC with side lengths a, b, and c, where a and b satisfy |a+b-6|+(a-b+4)^2=0, then the range of the length of the third side c is (　　)A: 3<c<5B: 2<c<4C: 4<c<6D: 5<c<6

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I need to solve this problem about the triangle ABC where the sides a and b satisfy the equation |a + b - 6| + (a - b + 4)^2 = 0, and then find the range of the third side c. The options are given as A to D. Hmm, let me think step by step.First, the equation given is |a + b - 6| + (a - b + 4)^2 = 0. Since both absolute value and square terms are non-negative, the only way their sum can be zero is if each term individually equals zero. That's a common trick in these problems. So, that means both |a + b - 6| = 0 and (a - b + 4)^2 = 0 must hold true simultaneously.Starting with the first equation: |a + b - 6| = 0. The absolute value of something is zero only when the expression inside is zero. So, a + b - 6 = 0, which simplifies to a + b = 6.Second equation: (a - b + 4)^2 = 0. Similarly, the square of a real number is zero only if the number itself is zero. So, a - b + 4 = 0, which simplifies to a - b = -4. Alternatively, that can be written as b - a = 4.Now, I have a system of two equations:1. a + b = 62. a - b = -4I can solve this system to find the values of a and b. Let me add the two equations together. Adding the left sides: a + b + a - b = 2a. Adding the right sides: 6 + (-4) = 2. So, 2a = 2 => a = 1.Then, substitute a = 1 into the first equation: 1 + b = 6 => b = 5.Wait, so a is 1 and b is 5? Let me check that with the second equation: a - b = 1 - 5 = -4, which matches the second equation. So that's correct. So the two sides a and b are 1 and 5.But wait, in a triangle, the sides must satisfy the triangle inequality. The sum of any two sides must be greater than the third side. So, the third side c must satisfy:1. a + b > c2. a + c > b3. b + c > aGiven that a = 1 and b = 5, substituting these into the inequalities:1. 1 + 5 > c => 6 > c => c < 62. 1 + c > 5 => c > 5 - 1 => c > 43. 5 + c > 1 => c > 1 - 5 => c > -4But since side lengths are positive, the third inequality (c > -4) is automatically satisfied. So the actual constraints are from the first two inequalities: c must be greater than 4 and less than 6. Therefore, the range of c is 4 < c < 6.Looking at the options given:A: 3 < c < 5B: 2 < c < 4C: 4 < c < 6D: 5 < c < 6So the correct answer should be option C. Let me just verify again to make sure I didn't make a mistake.We had the equation leading to a = 1 and b = 5, then triangle inequalities: sum of a and b is 6, so c must be less than 6. Then, for the other inequalities, since 5 is the larger side, c must be greater than 5 - 1 = 4. So yes, c has to be between 4 and 6. That matches option C.I think that's solid. No mistakes noticed here. So I feel confident that the answer is C.Final Answerboxed{C}</think>Given a triangle (ABC) with side lengths (a), (b), and (c), where (a) and (b) satisfy the equation (|a + b - 6| + (a - b + 4)^2 = 0), we need to find the range of the length of the third side (c).First, we solve the given equation. ((a - b + 4)^2 = 0) implies (a - b = -4).Solving the system of equations:1. (a + b = 6)2. (a - b = -4)Adding these equations, we get:[(a + b) + (a - b) = 6 + (-4) implies 2a = 2 implies a = 1]Substituting (a = 1) into (a + b = 6):[1 + b = 6 implies b = 5]Thus, the sides (a = 1) and (b = 5). 🔑:Solution: Since |a+b-6|+(a-b+4)^2=0, it follows that a+b=6 and b-a=4. Therefore, the range of the length of the third side c is 4<c<6. Hence, the correct option is boxed{text{C}}.
